<p><b>Hagia Sophia</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɑː/ 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/oʊ/ long 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>; from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Ἁγία Σοφία</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aˈʝia soˈfia]</span>), "<a href="/wiki/Sophia_(wisdom)#Christianity" title="Sophia (wisdom)">Holy Wisdom</a>"; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la" xml:lang="la"><i>Sancta Sophia</i></span> or <i>Sancta Sapientia</i>; <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>: <span lang="tr" xml:lang="tr"><i>Ayasofya</i></span>) was a Greek Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">patriarchal</a> <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a> (<a href="/wiki/Church_(building)" title="Church (building)">church</a>), later an <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">imperial</a> <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosque</a>, and now a museum (Ayasofya Müzesi) in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>. From the date of its construction in 537 AD, and until 1453, it served as an <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedral</a> and seat of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">Patriarch of Constantinople</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw112_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw112-1">[1]</a></sup> except between 1204 and 1261, when it was converted by the Fourth Crusaders to a <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> cathedral under the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a>. The building was later converted into an Ottoman mosque from 29 May 1453 until 1931. It was then <a href="/wiki/Secularity" title="Secularity">secularized</a> and opened as a museum on 1 February 1935.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Famous in particular for its massive <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">dome</a>, it is considered the epitome of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAT_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-BAT-3">[3]</a></sup> and is said to have "changed the history of architecture".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-4">[4]</a></sup> It remained the world's largest cathedral for nearly a thousand years, until <a href="/wiki/Seville_Cathedral" title="Seville Cathedral">Seville Cathedral</a> was completed in 1520.</p>
<p>The current building was originally constructed as a church between 532 and 537 on the orders of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> and was the third Church of the Holy Wisdom to occupy the site, the previous two having been destroyed by rioters. It was designed by the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> geometers <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_of_Tralles" title="Anthemius of Tralles">Anthemius of Tralles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The church was <a href="/wiki/Consecrations_in_Eastern_Christianity" title="Consecrations in Eastern Christianity">dedicated</a> to the <i><a href="/wiki/Sophia_(wisdom)" title="Sophia (wisdom)">Wisdom of God</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Logos_(Christianity)" title="Logos (Christianity)">Logos</a>, the second <a href="/wiki/Hypostasis_(philosophy_and_religion)" title="Hypostasis (philosophy and religion)">person</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja471_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-ja471-6">[6]</a></sup> its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patronal_feast" title="Patronal feast">patronal feast</a> taking place on 25 December, the <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">commemoration of the birth</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">incarnation</a> of the Logos in Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja471_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-ja471-6">[6]</a></sup> Although sometimes referred to as Sancta Sophia (as though it were named after <a href="/wiki/Sophia_the_Martyr" title="Sophia the Martyr">Sophia the Martyr</a>), <i>sophia</i> being the phonetic spelling in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> of the Greek word for wisdom, its full name in Greek is <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Ναὸς τῆς Ἁγίας τοῦ Θεοῦ Σοφίας</span>, <i>Naos tēs Hagias tou Theou Sophias</i>, "Shrine of the Holy Wisdom of God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKenzieGraham1998_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzieGraham1998-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binns2002_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Binns2002-8">[8]</a></sup> The church contained a large collection of <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> and featured, among other things, a 15-metre (49 ft) silver <a href="/wiki/Iconostasis" title="Iconostasis">iconostasis</a>. The focal point of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> for nearly one thousand years, the building witnessed the <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a> of Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Michael_I_Cerularius" title="Michael I Cerularius">Michael I Cerularius</a> on the part of <a href="/wiki/Humbert_of_Silva_Candida" title="Humbert of Silva Candida">Humbert of Silva Candida</a>, the papal envoy of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_IX" title="Pope Leo IX">Pope Leo IX</a> in 1054, an act that is commonly considered the start of the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a>.</p>
<p>In 1453, <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Constantinople was conquered</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> under <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_the_Conqueror" title="Mehmed the Conqueror">Mehmed the Conqueror</a>, who ordered this main church of Orthodox Christianity converted into a mosque. By that point, the church had fallen into a state of disrepair. Nevertheless, the Christian cathedral made a strong impression on the new Ottoman rulers and they decided to convert it into a mosque.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-http:.2F.2Fwww.livescience.com.2F27574-hagia-sophia.html_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-http:.2F.2Fwww.livescience.com.2F27574-hagia-sophia.html-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archnet.org_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-archnet.org-10">[10]</a></sup> The bells, altar, iconostasis, and sacrificial vessels and other relics were destroyed and the <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaics</a> depicting Jesus, his Mother Mary, Christian saints and angels were also destroyed or plastered over. <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic features</a>—such as the <a href="/wiki/Mihrab" title="Mihrab">mihrab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minbar" title="Minbar">minbar</a>, and four <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minarets</a>—were added. It remained a mosque until 1931, when it was closed to the public for four years. It was re-opened in 1935 as a museum by the Republic of <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>. Hagia Sophia was, as of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the second-most visited museum in Turkey, attracting almost 3.3 million visitors annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mus_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-mus-11">[11]</a></sup> According to data released by the Turkish Culture and Tourism Ministry, Hagia Sophia was Turkey’s most visited tourist attraction in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>From its initial conversion until the construction of the nearby <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Ahmed_Mosque" title="Sultan Ahmed Mosque">Sultan Ahmed Mosque</a> (Blue Mosque of Istanbul) in 1616, it was the principal mosque of Istanbul. The Byzantine architecture of the Hagia Sophia served as inspiration for many other Ottoman mosques, such as the aforementioned mosque, the <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Eehzade_Mosque" title="Şehzade Mosque">Şehzade Mosque</a>, the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%BCleymaniye_Mosque" title="Süleymaniye Mosque">Süleymaniye Mosque</a>, the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%BCstem_Pasha_Mosque" title="Rüstem Pasha Mosque">Rüstem Pasha Mosque</a> and the <a href="/wiki/K%C4%B1l%C4%B1%C3%A7_Ali_Pasha_Complex" title="Kılıç Ali Pasha Complex">Kılıç Ali Pasha Complex</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_of_Constantius_II">Church of Constantius II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Church of Constantius II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first church on the site was known as the <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Μεγάλη Ἐκκλησία</span> (<i>Megálē Ekklēsíā</i>, "Great Church"), or in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> "Magna Ecclesia",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw84_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw84-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Great_Church_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Great_Church-14">[14]</a></sup> because of its larger dimensions in comparison to the contemporary churches in the City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja471_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-ja471-6">[6]</a></sup> Inaugurated on 15 February 360 (during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Constantius_II" title="Constantius II">Constantius II</a>) by the <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arian</a> bishop <a href="/wiki/Eudoxius_of_Antioch" title="Eudoxius of Antioch">Eudoxius of Antioch</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja472_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-ja472-15">[15]</a></sup> it was built next to the area where the imperial palace was being developed. The nearby <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Irene" title="Hagia Irene">Hagia Eirene</a> ("Holy Peace") church was completed earlier and served as cathedral until the Great Church was completed. Both churches acted together as the principal churches of the Byzantine Empire.</p>
<p>Writing in 440, <a href="/wiki/Socrates_of_Constantinople" title="Socrates of Constantinople">Socrates of Constantinople</a> claimed that the church was built by <a href="/wiki/Constantius_II" title="Constantius II">Constantius II</a>, who was working on it in 346.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja472_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-ja472-15">[15]</a></sup> A tradition which is not older than the 7th or 8th century, reports that the edifice was built by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine the Great</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja472_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-ja472-15">[15]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zonaras" title="Zonaras">Zonaras</a> reconciles the two opinions, writing that Constantius had repaired the edifice consecrated by <a href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Nicomedia" title="Eusebius of Nicomedia">Eusebius of Nicomedia</a>, after it had collapsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja472_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-ja472-15">[15]</a></sup> Since Eusebius was bishop of Constantinople from 339 to 341, and Constantine died in 337, it seems possible that the first church was erected by the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja472_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-ja472-15">[15]</a></sup> The edifice was built as a traditional Latin colonnaded basilica with galleries and a wooden roof. It was preceded by an <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(architecture)" title="Atrium (architecture)">atrium</a>. It was claimed to be one of the world's most outstanding monuments at the time.</p>
<p>The Patriarch of Constantinople <a href="/wiki/John_Chrysostom" title="John Chrysostom">John Chrysostom</a> came into a conflict with Empress <a href="/wiki/Aelia_Eudoxia" title="Aelia Eudoxia">Aelia Eudoxia</a>, wife of the emperor <a href="/wiki/Arcadius" title="Arcadius">Arcadius</a>, and was sent into exile on 20 June 404. During the subsequent riots, this first church was largely burned down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ja472_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-ja472-15">[15]</a></sup> Nothing remains of the first church today.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_of_Theodosius_II">Church of Theodosius II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Church of Theodosius II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A second church on the site was ordered by <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a>, who inaugurated it on 10 October 415. The basilica with a wooden roof was built by architect Rufinus. A fire started during the tumult of the <a href="/wiki/Nika_riots" title="Nika riots">Nika Revolt</a> and burned the second Hagia Sophia to the ground on 13–14 January 532.</p>
<p>Several marble blocks from the second church survive to the present; among them are <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">reliefs</a> depicting 12 lambs representing the 12 apostles. Originally part of a monumental front entrance, they now reside in an excavation pit adjacent to the museum's entrance after they were discovered in 1935 beneath the western courtyard by A. M. Schneider. Further digging was forsaken for fear of impinging on the integrity of the building.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Basilica_of_the_Hagia_Sophia_.28current_structure.29">Basilica of the Hagia Sophia (current structure)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Basilica of the Hagia Sophia (current structure)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:38-manasses-chronicle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1088" data-file-width="2023" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/38-manasses-chronicle.jpg/220px-38-manasses-chronicle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/38-manasses-chronicle.jpg/330px-38-manasses-chronicle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/38-manasses-chronicle.jpg/440px-38-manasses-chronicle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:38-manasses-chronicle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Construction of church depicted in codex Manasses Chronicle (14th century)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 23 February 532, only a few weeks after the destruction of the second basilica, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> decided to build a third and entirely different basilica, larger and more majestic than its predecessors.</p>
<p>Justinian chose physicist <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a> and mathematician <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_of_Tralles" title="Anthemius of Tralles">Anthemius of Tralles</a> as architects; Anthemius, however, died within the first year of the endeavor. The construction is described in the Byzantine historian <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">Procopius</a>' <i>On Buildings</i> (<i>Peri ktismatōn</i>, Latin: <i>De aedificiis</i>). Columns and other marbles were brought from all over the empire, throughout the Mediterranean. The idea of these columns being spoils from cities such as Rome and Ephesus is a later invention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Even though they were made specifically for Hagia Sophia, the columns show variations in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> More than ten thousand people were employed. This new church was contemporaneously recognized as a major work of architecture. The theories of <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_Alexandria" title="Hero of Alexandria">Heron of Alexandria</a> may have been utilized to address the challenges presented by building such an expansive dome over so large a space.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The emperor, together with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Menas_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Menas of Constantinople">Patriarch Menas</a>, inaugurated the new basilica on 27 December 537 – 5 years and 10 months after construction start – with much pomp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw86_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw86-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The mosaics inside the church were, however, only completed under the reign of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justin_II" title="Justin II">Justin II</a> (565–578).</p>
<p>Hagia Sophia was the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">Orthodox patriarch of Constantinople</a> and a principal setting for Byzantine imperial ceremonies, such as coronations. Like other churches throughout <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>, the basilica offered <a href="/wiki/Right_of_asylum" title="Right of asylum">sanctuary from persecution</a> to outlaws.</p>
<p>Earthquakes in August 553 and on <a href="/wiki/557_Constantinople_earthquake" title="557 Constantinople earthquake">14 December 557</a> caused cracks in the main dome and eastern half-dome. The main dome collapsed completely during a subsequent earthquake on 7 May 558,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jan_1_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jan_1-21">[21]</a></sup> destroying the <a href="/wiki/Ambon_(liturgy)" title="Ambon (liturgy)">ambon</a>, altar, and <a href="/wiki/Ciborium_(architecture)" title="Ciborium (architecture)">ciborium</a>. The collapse was due mainly to the unfeasibly high bearing load and to the enormous shearing load of the dome, which was too flat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw86_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-mw86-18">[18]</a></sup> These caused the deformation of the piers which sustained the dome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw86_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-mw86-18">[18]</a></sup> The emperor ordered an immediate restoration. He entrusted it to Isidorus the Younger, nephew of Isidore of Miletus, who used lighter materials and elevated the dome by "30 feet"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw86_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-mw86-18">[18]</a></sup> (about 6.25 meters or 20.5 feet)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="is it 20 or 30 feet? (May 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> – giving the building its current interior height of 55.6 meters (182 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Moreover, Isidorus changed the dome type, erecting a ribbed dome with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pendentives" title="Pendentives">pendentives</a>, whose diameter lay between 32.7 and 33.5 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw86_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-mw86-18">[18]</a></sup> Under Justinian's orders, eight <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_order" title="Corinthian order">Corinthian columns</a> were disassembled from <a href="/wiki/Baalbek" title="Baalbek">Baalbek</a>, Lebanon, and shipped to Constantinople around 560.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> This reconstruction, giving the church its present 6th-century form, was completed in 562. The Byzantine poet <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Silentiary" title="Paul the Silentiary">Paul the Silentiary</a> composed a long epic poem (still extant), known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecphrasis" title="Ecphrasis">Ekphrasis</a></i>, for the rededication of the basilica presided over by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Eutychius_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Eutychius of Constantinople">Patriarch Eutychius</a> on 23 December 562.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_036_(6498284165).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1620" data-file-width="1080" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Istanbul_036_%286498284165%29.jpg/170px-Istanbul_036_%286498284165%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Istanbul_036_%286498284165%29.jpg/255px-Istanbul_036_%286498284165%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Istanbul_036_%286498284165%29.jpg/340px-Istanbul_036_%286498284165%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_036_(6498284165).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The vaulting of the nave. <i>(<a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Istanbul_036_(6498284165).jpg" title="commons:File:Istanbul 036 (6498284165).jpg">annotations</a></i>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 726, the emperor <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo the Isaurian</a> issued a series of edicts against the veneration of images, ordering the army to destroy all icons – ushering in the period of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Iconoclasm" title="Byzantine Iconoclasm">Byzantine iconoclasm</a>. At that time, all religious pictures and statues were removed from the Hagia Sophia. After a brief reprieve under Empress <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irene_(empress)" title="Irene (empress)">Irene</a> (797–802), the iconoclasts made a comeback. Emperor <a href="/wiki/Theophilos_(emperor)" title="Theophilos (emperor)">Theophilus</a> (829–842) was strongly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic art</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> which forbids the representation of living beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> He had a two-winged bronze doors with his <a href="/wiki/Monogram" title="Monogram">monograms</a> installed at the southern entrance of the church.</p>
<p>The basilica suffered damage, first in a great fire in 859, and again in an earthquake on 8 January 869, that made one of the half-domes collapse. Emperor <a href="/wiki/Basil_I" title="Basil I">Basil I</a> ordered the church repaired.</p>
<p>After the great earthquake of 25 October 989, which collapsed the Western dome arch, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a> asked for the Armenian architect <a href="/wiki/Trdat_the_Architect" title="Trdat the Architect">Trdat</a>, creator of the cathedrals of <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Ani" title="Cathedral of Ani">Ani</a> and Argina, to direct the repairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> He erected again and reinforced the fallen dome arch, and rebuilt the west side of the dome with 15 dome ribs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw87_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw87-27">[27]</a></sup> The extent of the damage required six years of repair and reconstruction; the church was re-opened on 13 May 994. At the end of the reconstruction, the church's decorations were renovated, including the addition of four immense paintings of cherubs; a new depiction of Christ on the dome; a burial cloth of Christ shown on Fridays, and on the apse a new depiction of the Virgin Mary holding Jesus, between the apostles Peter and Paul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ma287_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ma287-28">[28]</a></sup> On the great side arches were painted the prophets and the teachers of the church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ma287_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-ma287-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his book <i><a href="/wiki/De_Ceremoniis" title="De Ceremoniis">De caerimoniis aulae Byzantinae</a></i> ("Book of Ceremonies"), Emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_VII" title="Constantine VII">Constantine VII</a> (913–919) wrote a detailed account of the ceremonies held in the Hagia Sophia by the emperor and the patriarch.</p>
<p>Upon the capture of Constantinople during the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a>, the church was ransacked and desecrated by the Crusaders, as described by the Byzantine historian <a href="/wiki/Niketas_Choniates" title="Niketas Choniates">Niketas Choniates</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin occupation of Constantinople</a> (1204–1261) the church became a Roman Catholic cathedral. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baldwin_I_of_Constantinople" title="Baldwin I of Constantinople">Baldwin I of Constantinople</a> was crowned emperor on 16 May 1204 in Hagia Sophia, at a ceremony which closely followed Byzantine practices. <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Dandolo" title="Enrico Dandolo">Enrico Dandolo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Doge_of_Venice" title="Doge of Venice">Doge</a> of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a> who commanded the sack and invasion of the city by the Latin Crusaders in 1204, is buried inside the church, probably in the upper Eastern gallery. In the 19th century an Italian restoration team placed a <a href="/wiki/Cenotaph" title="Cenotaph">cenotaph</a> marker near the probable location, which is still visible today. The marker is frequently mistaken by tourists as being a medieval marker of the actual tomb of the doge. The real tomb was destroyed by the Ottomans after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">conquest of Constantinople in 1453</a> and subsequent conversion of Hagia Sophia into a mosque.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the recapture in 1261 by the Byzantines, the church was in a dilapidated state. In 1317, emperor <a href="/wiki/Andronikos_II_Palaiologos" title="Andronikos II Palaiologos">Andronicus II</a> ordered four new buttresses (Πυραμίδας, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Language" title="Greek Language">Greek</a>: "Pyramídas") to be built in the eastern and northern parts of the church, financing them with the inheritance of his deceased wife, <a href="/wiki/Irene_of_Montferrat" title="Irene of Montferrat">Irene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup> New cracks developed in the dome after the earthquake of October 1344, and several parts of the building collapsed on 19 May 1346; consequently, the church was closed until 1354, when repairs were undertaken by architects Astras and Peralta.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mosque_.281453.E2.80.931935.29">Mosque (1453–1935)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mosque (1453–1935)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> fell to the attacking Ottoman forces on the 29th of May in 1453. In accordance with the traditional custom at the time, Sultan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mehmet_II" title="Mehmet II">Mehmet II</a> allowed his troops and his entourage three full days of unbridled pillage and looting in the city shortly after it was captured. Once the three days passed, he would then claim its remaining contents for himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runciman_1_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman_1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicol_a_1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicol_a_1-32">[32]</a></sup> Hagia Sophia was not exempted from the pillage and looting and specifically became its focal point as the invaders believed it to contain the greatest treasures and valuables of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicol_a_2_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicol_a_2-33">[33]</a></sup> Shortly after Constantinople's defenses collapsed and the Ottoman troops entered the city victoriously, the pillagers and looters made their way to the Hagia Sophia and battered down its doors before storming in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runciman_2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman_2-34">[34]</a></sup> All throughout the period of the siege of Constantinople, the trapped worshipers of the city participated in the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a> and Prayer of the Hours at the Hagia Sophia and the church formed a safe-haven and a refuge for many of those who were unable to contribute to the city's defense, which comprised women, children, the elderly and the sick and the wounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runciman_3_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman_3-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicol_b_1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicol_b_1-36">[36]</a></sup> Being hopelessly trapped in the church, the many congregants and yet more refugees inside became spoils-of-war to be divided amongst the triumphant invaders. The building was significantly desecrated and looted to a large extent, with the helpless occupants who sought shelter within the church being either enslaved, physically and sexually violated or simply slaughtered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicol_a_2_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nicol_a_2-33">[33]</a></sup> While most of the elderly and the infirm/wounded and sick were killed, a vast number of women and girls were raped and the remainder (mainly teenage males and young boys) were chained up and sold off into slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runciman_2_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman_2-34">[34]</a></sup> The church's priests and religious personnel continued to perform Christian rites, prayers and ceremonies until finally being forced to stop by the invaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runciman_2_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman_2-34">[34]</a></sup> When Sultan Mehmet II and his accompanying entourage entered the church, he insisted that it should be converted into a mosque at once. One of the <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">ulama</a> present then climbed up the church's pulpit and recited out the <a href="/wiki/Shahada" title="Shahada">Shahada</a>, thus marking the beginning of the gradual conversion of the church into a mosque.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runciman_5_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman_5-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fountain_Hagia_Sophia_2007_002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="1528" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Fountain_Hagia_Sophia_2007_002.jpg/220px-Fountain_Hagia_Sophia_2007_002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Fountain_Hagia_Sophia_2007_002.jpg/330px-Fountain_Hagia_Sophia_2007_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Fountain_Hagia_Sophia_2007_002.jpg/440px-Fountain_Hagia_Sophia_2007_002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fountain_Hagia_Sophia_2007_002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fountain (<i>Şadırvan</i>) for ritual ablutions</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As described by several Western visitors (such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoban</a> nobleman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pero_Tafur" title="Pero Tafur">Pero Tafur</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florentine</a> <a href="/wiki/Cristoforo_Buondelmonti" title="Cristoforo Buondelmonti">Cristoforo Buondelmonti</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> the church was in a dilapidated state, with several of its doors fallen from their hinges; Mehmed II ordered a renovation as well as the conversion. Mehmet attended the first Friday prayer in the mosque on 1 June 1453.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mb288_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-mb288-40">[40]</a></sup> Aya Sofya became the first imperial mosque of Istanbul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ne13_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-ne13-41">[41]</a></sup> To the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">Waqf</a> were endowed most of the existing houses in the city and the area of the future <a href="/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace" title="Topkapı Palace">Topkapı Palace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup> From 1478, 2,360 shops, 1,300 houses, 4 <a href="/wiki/Caravanserai" title="Caravanserai">caravanserais</a>, 30 <i><a href="/wiki/Boza" title="Boza">boza</a></i> shops, and 23 shops of sheep heads and trotters gave their income to the foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bo145_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-bo145-42">[42]</a></sup> Through the imperial charters of 1520 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anno_Hegirae" title="Anno Hegirae">AH</a> 926) and 1547 (AH 954) shops and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Bazaar,_Istanbul" title="Grand Bazaar, Istanbul">Grand Bazaar</a> and other markets were added to the foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before 1481 a small <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minaret</a> was erected on the southwest corner of the building, above the stair tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup> Later, the subsequent sultan, <a href="/wiki/Bayezid_II" title="Bayezid II">Bayezid II</a> (1481–1512), built another minaret at the northeast corner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup> One of these collapsed after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1509_Istanbul_earthquake" title="1509 Istanbul earthquake">earthquake of 1509</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-6"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup> and around the middle of the 16th century they were both replaced by two diagonally opposite minarets built at the east and west corners of the edifice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-7"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1043" data-file-width="929" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg/220px-Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg/330px-Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg/440px-Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i>mihrab</i> located in the apse where the altar used to stand, pointing towards Mecca</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 16th century the sultan <a href="/wiki/Suleiman_the_Magnificent" title="Suleiman the Magnificent">Suleiman the Magnificent</a> (1520–1566) brought back two colossal candlesticks from his conquest of <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>. They were placed on either side of the <i><a href="/wiki/Mihrab" title="Mihrab">mihrab</a></i>. During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Selim_II" title="Selim II">Selim II</a> (1566–1574), the building started showing signs of fatigue and was extensively strengthened with the addition of structural supports to its exterior by Ottoman architect <a href="/wiki/Mimar_Sinan" title="Mimar Sinan">Mimar Sinan</a>, who was also an earthquake engineer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earthquake_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-earthquake-43">[43]</a></sup> In addition to strengthening the historic Byzantine structure, Sinan built the two additional large minarets at the western end of the building, the original sultan's lodge, and the <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCrbe" title="Türbe">Türbe</a> (mausoleum) of Selim II to the southeast of the building in 1576-7 / AH 984. In order to do that, parts of the Patriarchate at the south corner of the building were pulled down the previous year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-8"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup> Moreover, the golden <a href="/wiki/Crescent#In_the_Islamic_World" title="Crescent">crescent</a> was mounted on the top of the dome,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-9"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup> while a respect zone 35 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_Turkish_units_of_measurement" title="Traditional Turkish units of measurement">arşin</a></i> (about 24 m) wide was imposed around the building, pulling down all the houses which in the meantime had nested around it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-10"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup> Later his türbe hosted also 43 tombs of Ottoman princes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-11"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1594 / AH 1004 <i>Mimar</i> (court architect) <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mimar_Davud_A%C4%9Fa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mimar Davud Ağa (page does not exist)">Davud Ağa</a> built the türbe of <a href="/wiki/Murad_III" title="Murad III">Murad III</a> (1574–1595), where the Sultan and his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valide_Sultan" title="Valide Sultan">Valide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safiye_Sultan" title="Safiye Sultan">Safiye Sultan</a> were later buried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-12"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup> The octagonal mausoleum of their son <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_III" title="Mehmed III">Mehmed III</a> (1595–1603) and his Valide was built next to it in 1608 / AH 1017 by royal architect Dalgiç Mehmet Aĝa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw93_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw93-44">[44]</a></sup> His son <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_I" title="Mustafa I">Mustafa I</a> (1617–1618; 1622–1623) converted the baptistery into his türbe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw93_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-mw93-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Murad III had also two large <a href="/wiki/Alabaster" title="Alabaster">alabaster</a> Hellenistic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urns" title="Urns">urns</a> transported from <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">Pergamon</a> and placed on two sides of the nave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-13"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1717, under Sultan <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_III" title="Ahmed III">Ahmed III</a> (1703–1730), the crumbling plaster of the interior was renovated, contributing indirectly to the preservation of many mosaics, which otherwise would have been destroyed by mosque workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw93_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-mw93-44">[44]</a></sup> In fact, it was usual for them to sell mosaics stones – believed to be <a href="/wiki/Talisman" title="Talisman">talismans</a> – to the visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw93_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-mw93-44">[44]</a></sup> Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_I" title="Mahmud I">Mahmud I</a> ordered the restoration of the building in 1739 and added a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madrasah" title="Madrasah">medrese</a></i> (a Koranic school, now the library of the museum), an <i><a href="/wiki/Imaret" title="Imaret">Imaret</a></i> (soup kitchen for distribution to the poor) and a library, and in 1740 a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sadirvan" title="Sadirvan">Şadirvan</a></i> (fountain for ritual ablutions), thus transforming it into a <i><a href="/wiki/K%C3%BClliye" title="Külliye">külliye</a></i>, i.e. a social complex. At the same time a new sultan's lodge and a new mihrab were built inside.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Renovation_of_1847">Renovation of 1847</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Renovation of 1847">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Restoration of the Hagia Sophia was ordered by Sultan <a href="/wiki/Abd%C3%BClmecid_I" title="Abdülmecid I">Abdülmecid</a> and completed by eight hundred workers between 1847 and 1849, under the supervision of the <a href="/wiki/Ticino" title="Ticino">Swiss-Italian</a> architect brothers <a href="/wiki/Fossati_brothers" title="Fossati brothers">Gaspare and Giuseppe Fossati</a>. The brothers consolidated the dome and vaults, straightened the columns, and revised the decoration of the exterior and the interior of the building. The mosaics in the upper gallery were exposed and cleaned, although many were re-covered "for protection against further damage". The old <a href="/wiki/Chandelier" title="Chandelier">chandeliers</a> were replaced by new pendant ones. New gigantic circular-framed disks or medallions were hung on columns. These were inscribed with the names of <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, the first four caliphs <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthman_Ibn_Affan" title="Uthman Ibn Affan">Uthman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a>, and the two grandchildren of Muhammad: <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">Hassan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">Hussain</a>, by the calligrapher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kazasker_Mustafa_%C4%B0zzed_Effendi" title="Kazasker Mustafa İzzed Effendi">Kazasker Mustafa İzzed Effendi</a> (1801–1877). In 1850 the architects Fossati built a new sultan's lodge or loge in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Byzantine_architecture" title="Neo-Byzantine architecture">Neo-Byzantine style</a> connected to the royal pavilion behind the mosque. They also renovated the <a href="/wiki/Minbar" title="Minbar">minbar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mihrab" title="Mihrab">mihrab</a>. Outside the main building, the minarets were repaired and altered so that they were of equal height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> A timekeeper's building and a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madrasah" title="Madrasah">madrasah</a> were built. When the restoration was finished, the mosque was re-opened with ceremonial pomp on 13 July 1849.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Museum_.281935.E2.80.93present.29">Museum (1935–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Museum (1935–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Dome.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="1294" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Dome.jpg/170px-Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Dome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Dome.jpg/255px-Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Dome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Dome.jpg/340px-Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Dome.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Interior_Dome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The interior undergoing restoration</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1935, the first Turkish President and founder of the Republic of Turkey, <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">Mustafa Kemal Atatürk</a>, transformed the building into a museum. The carpets were removed and marble floor decorations such as the <a href="/wiki/Omphalion" title="Omphalion">Omphalion</a> appeared for the first time in centuries, while the white plaster covering many of the mosaics was removed. Nevertheless, the condition of the structure deteriorated, and the <a href="/wiki/World_Monuments_Fund" title="World Monuments Fund">World Monuments Fund</a> placed Hagia Sophia on <a href="/wiki/1996_World_Monuments_Watch" title="1996 World Monuments Watch">1996 World Monuments Watch</a>, and again in <a href="/wiki/1998_World_Monuments_Watch" title="1998 World Monuments Watch">1998</a>. The building's copper roof had cracked, causing water to leak down over the fragile frescoes and mosaics. Moisture entered from below as well. Rising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_water" title="Ground water">ground water</a> had raised the level of humidity within the monument, creating an unstable environment for stone and paint. The WMF secured a series of grants from 1997 to 2002 for the restoration of the dome. The first stage of work involved the structural stabilization and repair of the cracked roof, which was undertaken with the participation of the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Culture_and_Tourism_(Turkey)" title="Ministry of Culture and Tourism (Turkey)">Turkish Ministry of Culture</a>. The second phase, the preservation of the dome's interior, afforded the opportunity to employ and train young Turkish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservator_(museum)" title="Conservator (museum)">conservators</a> in the care of mosaics. By 2006, the WMF project was complete, though many other areas of Hagia Sophia continue to require significant stability improvement, restoration and conservation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Haghia Sophia is currently (2014) the second most visited museum in Turkey, attracting almost 3.3 million visitors annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mus_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-mus-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although use of the complex as a place of worship (mosque or church) was strictly prohibited,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> in 2006 the Turkish government allowed the allocation of a small room in the museum complex to be used as a prayer room for Christian and Muslim museum staff,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> and since 2013 from the minarets of the museum the muezzin sings the call to prayer twice per day, in the afternoon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, Greek American politician Chris Spirou launched an international organization "Free Agia Sophia Council" championing the cause of restoring the building to its original function as a Christian church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Since the early 2010s, several campaigns and government high officials, notably Turkey's deputy prime minister <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BClent_Ar%C4%B1n%C3%A7" title="Bülent Arınç">Bülent Arınç</a> in November 2013, have been demanding that Hagia Sophia be converted into a mosque again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In 2015, in retaliation for the acknowledgment by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Genocide" title="Armenian Genocide">Armenian Genocide</a>, the Mufti of Ankara, Mefail Hızlı, stated that he believes the conversion of Hagia Sophia into a mosque will be accelerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 1, 2016, Muslim prayers were held again in the Hagia Sophia for the first time in 85 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia-Sophia-Laengsschnitt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1912" data-file-width="3996" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Hagia-Sophia-Laengsschnitt.jpg/260px-Hagia-Sophia-Laengsschnitt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Hagia-Sophia-Laengsschnitt.jpg/390px-Hagia-Sophia-Laengsschnitt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Hagia-Sophia-Laengsschnitt.jpg/520px-Hagia-Sophia-Laengsschnitt.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia-Sophia-Laengsschnitt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Section of a "restored" design</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia-Sophia-Grundriss.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2467" data-file-width="4173" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hagia-Sophia-Grundriss.jpg/260px-Hagia-Sophia-Grundriss.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hagia-Sophia-Grundriss.jpg/390px-Hagia-Sophia-Grundriss.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hagia-Sophia-Grundriss.jpg/520px-Hagia-Sophia-Grundriss.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia-Sophia-Grundriss.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
a) Plan of the gallery (upper half)<br/>
b) Plan of the ground floor (lower half)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hagia Sophia is one of the greatest surviving examples of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAT_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-BAT-3">[3]</a></sup> Its interior is decorated with <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> pillars and coverings of great artistic value. The temple itself was so richly and artistically decorated that Justinian proclaimed, "<a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Solomon</a>, I have outdone thee!" (Νενίκηκά σε Σολομών). Justinian himself had overseen the completion of the greatest cathedral ever built up to that time, and it was to remain the largest cathedral for 1,000 years up until the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Seville_Cathedral" title="Seville Cathedral">cathedral in Seville</a> in Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Justinian's basilica was at once the culminating architectural achievement of <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">late antiquity</a> and the first masterpiece of Byzantine architecture. Its influence, both architecturally and liturgically, was widespread and enduring in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a>, Roman Catholic, and Muslim worlds alike.</p>
<p>The vast interior has a complex structure. The <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a> is covered by a central dome which at its maximum is 55.6 m (182 ft 5 in) from floor level and rests on an arcade of 40 arched windows. Repairs to its structure have left the dome somewhat elliptical, with the diameter varying between 31.24 and 30.86 m (102 ft 6 in and 101 ft 3 in).</p>
<p>At the western entrance side and eastern liturgical side, there are arched openings extended by half domes of identical diameter to the central dome, carried on smaller <a href="/wiki/Semi-dome" title="Semi-dome">semi-domed</a> <a href="/wiki/Exedra" title="Exedra">exedras</a>; a hierarchy of dome-headed elements built up to create a vast oblong interior crowned by the central dome, with a clear span of 76.2 m (250 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAT_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-BAT-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Interior surfaces are sheathed with polychrome marbles, green and white with purple <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(geology)" title="Porphyry (geology)">porphyry</a>, and gold mosaics. The exterior, clad in <a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">stucco</a>, was tinted yellow and red during restorations in the 19th century at the direction of the Fossati architects.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Narthex_and_portals">Narthex and portals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Narthex and portals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Imperial Gate was the main entrance between the exo- and esonarthex. It was reserved exclusively for the Emperor. The Byzantine mosaic above the portal depicts Christ and an unnamed emperor. A long ramp from the northern part of the outer narthex leads up to the upper gallery.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_Gallery">Upper Gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Upper Gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul.Hagia_Sophia055.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Istanbul.Hagia_Sophia055.jpg/220px-Istanbul.Hagia_Sophia055.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Istanbul.Hagia_Sophia055.jpg/330px-Istanbul.Hagia_Sophia055.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Istanbul.Hagia_Sophia055.jpg/440px-Istanbul.Hagia_Sophia055.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul.Hagia_Sophia055.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
West side of the upper gallery</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The upper gallery is laid out in a horseshoe shape that encloses the nave until the apse. Several mosaics are preserved in the upper gallery, an area traditionally reserved for the Empress and her court. The best-preserved mosaics are located in the southern part of the gallery.</p>
<p>The upper gallery contains <a href="/wiki/Runic_inscriptions_in_Hagia_Sophia" title="Runic inscriptions in Hagia Sophia">runic graffiti</a> presumed to be from the <a href="/wiki/Varangian_Guard" title="Varangian Guard">Varangian Guard</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dome">Dome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Dome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_001.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Istanbul_001.JPG/220px-Istanbul_001.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Istanbul_001.JPG/330px-Istanbul_001.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Istanbul_001.JPG/440px-Istanbul_001.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_001.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cupola" title="Cupola">Cupola</a> <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">dome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semi-dome" title="Semi-dome">semi-dome</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Apse" title="Apse">apse</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Le_24feb_a_11h23_-_Ste_Sophie_(1280x1280).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1280" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Le_24feb_a_11h23_-_Ste_Sophie_%281280x1280%29.jpg/220px-Le_24feb_a_11h23_-_Ste_Sophie_%281280x1280%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Le_24feb_a_11h23_-_Ste_Sophie_%281280x1280%29.jpg/330px-Le_24feb_a_11h23_-_Ste_Sophie_%281280x1280%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Le_24feb_a_11h23_-_Ste_Sophie_%281280x1280%29.jpg/440px-Le_24feb_a_11h23_-_Ste_Sophie_%281280x1280%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Le_24feb_a_11h23_-_Ste_Sophie_(1280x1280).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
See commons file for annotations</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">dome</a> of Hagia Sophia has spurred particular interest for many art historians, architects and engineers because of the innovative way the original architects envisioned it. The dome is carried on four spherical triangular <a href="/wiki/Pendentive" title="Pendentive">pendentives</a>, an element which was first fully realized in this building. The pendentives implement the transition from the circular base of the dome to the rectangular base below,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> restraining the lateral forces of the dome and allow its weight to flow downwards. They were reinforced with buttresses during Byzantine and later during Ottoman times, under the guidance of the architect <a href="/wiki/Mimar_Sinan" title="Mimar Sinan">Sinan</a>.</p>
<p>The weight of the dome remained a problem for most of the building's existence. The original cupola collapsed entirely after the earthquake of 558; in 563 a new dome was built by Isidore the younger, a nephew of Isidore of Miletus. Unlike the original, this included 40 ribs and was slightly taller, in order to lower the lateral forces on the church walls. A larger section of the second dome collapsed as well, in two episodes, so that today only two sections of the present dome, in the north and south side, still date from the 562 reconstruction. Of the whole dome's 40 ribs, the surviving north section contains 8 ribs, while the south section includes 6 ribs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although this design stabilizes the dome and the surrounding walls and arches, the actual construction of the walls of Hagia Sophia weakened the overall structure. The <a href="/wiki/Bricklayer" title="Bricklayer">bricklayers</a> used more <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">mortar</a> than brick, weakening the walls. The structure would have been more stable if the builders at least let the mortar cure before they began the next layer; however, they did not do this. When the dome was erected, its weight caused the walls to lean outward because of the wet mortar underneath. When Isidore the Younger rebuilt the fallen cupola, he had first to build up the interior of the walls to make them vertical again. Additionally, the architect raised the height of the rebuilt dome by approximately six m (20 feet) so that the lateral forces would not be as strong and its weight would be transmitted more effectively down into the walls. Moreover, he shaped the new cupola like a <a href="/wiki/Scallop" title="Scallop">scalloped</a> shell or the inside of an umbrella, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ogival_arch" title="Ogival arch">ribs</a> that extend from the top down to the base. These ribs allow the weight of the dome to flow between the windows, down the pendentives, and ultimately to the foundation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Hagia Sophia is famous for the light that reflects everywhere in the interior of the nave, giving the dome the appearance of hovering above. This effect was achieved by inserting forty windows around the base of the original structure. Moreover, the insertion of the windows in the dome structure lowers its weight.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minarets">Minarets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Minarets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The minarets were an Ottoman addition, and not part of the original church's Byzantine design. One of the minarets (at southwest) was built from red brick while the other three were built from white limestone and sandstone, of which the slender northeast column was erected by Sultan <a href="/wiki/Bayezid_II" title="Bayezid II">Bayezid II</a> while the two larger minarets to the west were erected by Sultan <a href="/wiki/Selim_II" title="Selim II">Selim II</a> and designed by the famous Ottoman architect <a href="/wiki/Mimar_Sinan" title="Mimar Sinan">Mimar Sinan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_elements_and_decorations">Notable elements and decorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Notable elements and decorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Originally, under Justinian's reign, the interior decorations consisted of abstract designs on marble slabs on the walls and floors, as well as mosaics on the curving vaults. Of these mosaics, one can still see the two <a href="/wiki/Archangel" title="Archangel">archangels</a> <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_(archangel)" title="Michael (archangel)">Michael</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Spandrel" title="Spandrel">spandrels</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bema" title="Bema">bema</a>. There were already a few figurative decorations, as attested by the eulogy of <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Silentiary" title="Paul the Silentiary">Paul the Silentiary</a>. The spandrels of the gallery are revetted in <i><a href="/wiki/Opus_sectile" title="Opus sectile">opus sectile</a></i>, showing patterns and figures of flowers and birds in precisely cut pieces of white marble set against a background of black marble. In later stages figurative mosaics were added, which were destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclastic controversy</a> (726–843). Present mosaics are from the post-iconoclastic period. The number of treasures, relics and miracle-working, painted icons of the Hagia Sophia grew progressively richer into an amazing collection.</p>
<p>Apart from the mosaics, a large number of figurative decorations were added during the second half of the 9th century: an image of Christ in the central dome; Orthodox saints, prophets and <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympana</a> below; historical figures connected with this church, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Ignatius_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Ignatius of Constantinople">Patriarch Ignatius</a>; some scenes from the <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">gospel</a> in the galleries. <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a> let artists paint on each of the four pendentives a giant six-winged <a href="/wiki/Cherub" title="Cherub">Cherub</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ma287_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-ma287-28">[28]</a></sup> The Ottomans covered their face with a golden <a href="/wiki/Halo_(religious_iconography)" title="Halo (religious iconography)">halo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ma287_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-ma287-28">[28]</a></sup> but in 2009 one of them was restored to the original state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ro157_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ro157-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loge_of_the_Empress">Loge of the Empress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Loge of the Empress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Loge of the Empress is located in the centre of the upper enclosure, or gallery, of the Hagia Sophia. From there the empress and the court-ladies would watch the proceedings down below. A round, green stone marks the spot where the <a href="/wiki/Throne" title="Throne">throne</a> of the empress stood.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lustration_urns">Lustration urns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Lustration urns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two huge marble <a href="/wiki/Lustratio" title="Lustratio">lustration</a> (ritual purification) <a href="/wiki/Urn" title="Urn">urns</a> were brought from <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">Pergamon</a> during the reign of Sultan <a href="/wiki/Murad_III" title="Murad III">Murad III</a>. From the Hellenistic period, they are carved from single blocks of marble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw91_30-14"><a href="#cite_note-mw91-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marble_Door">Marble Door</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Marble Door">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Marble Door inside the Hagia Sophia is located in the southern upper enclosure, or gallery. It was used by the participants in <a href="/wiki/Synod" title="Synod">synods</a>, they entered and left the meeting chamber through this door.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wishing_column">Wishing column</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Wishing column">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the northwest of the building there is a column with a hole in the middle covered by bronze plates. This column goes by different names; the perspiring column, the wishing column, the sweating column or the crying column. The column is said to be damp when touched and have supernatural powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The legend states that since <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Thaumaturgus" title="Gregory Thaumaturgus">St. Gregory the Miracle Worker</a> appeared at the column in year 1200, the column is moist. It is believed that touching the moisture cures many illnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
