<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mosaics">Mosaics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mosaics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceiling_decorations_in_Hagia_Sofia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3000" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Ceiling_decorations_in_Hagia_Sofia.jpg/170px-Ceiling_decorations_in_Hagia_Sofia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Ceiling_decorations_in_Hagia_Sofia.jpg/255px-Ceiling_decorations_in_Hagia_Sofia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Ceiling_decorations_in_Hagia_Sofia.jpg/340px-Ceiling_decorations_in_Hagia_Sofia.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceiling_decorations_in_Hagia_Sofia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ceiling decoration showing original Christian cross still visible through the later aniconic decoration</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The church was richly decorated with mosaics throughout the centuries. They either depicted the Virgin Mother, Jesus, saints, or emperors and empresses. Other parts were decorated in a purely decorative style with geometric patterns.</p>
<p>The mosaics however for their most part date to after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Iconoclasm" title="Byzantine Iconoclasm">Byzantine Iconoclasm</a> of 800 AD.</p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Sack of Constantinople</a> in 1204, the Latin Crusaders vandalized valuable items in every important Byzantine structure of the city, including the golden mosaics of the Hagia Sophia. Many of these items were shipped to <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Doge_of_Venice" title="Doge of Venice">Doge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Dandolo" title="Enrico Dandolo">Enrico Dandolo</a>, had organized the invasion and sack of Constantinople after an agreement with Prince <a href="/wiki/Alexios_IV_Angelos" title="Alexios IV Angelos">Alexios Angelos</a>, the son of a deposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isaac_II_Angelus" title="Isaac II Angelus">Byzantine emperor</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="19th-century_restoration">19th-century restoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 19th-century restoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Following the building's conversion into a mosque in 1453, many of its mosaics were covered with plaster, due to Islam's ban on representational imagery. This process was not completed at once, and reports exist from the 17th century in which travellers note that they could still see Christian images in the former church. In 1847–49, the building was restored by two <a href="/wiki/Ticino" title="Ticino">Swiss-Italian</a> <a href="/wiki/Fossati_brothers" title="Fossati brothers">Fossati brothers</a>, Gaspare and Giuseppe, and Sultan <a href="/wiki/Abd%C3%BClmecid_I" title="Abdülmecid I">Abdülmecid</a> allowed them to also document any mosaics they might discover during this process which were later archived in Swiss libraries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> This work did not include repairing the mosaics and after recording the details about an image, the Fossatis painted it over again. The Fossatis restored the mosaics of the two <i>hexapteryga</i> (singular <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">ἑξαπτέρυγον</span>, pr. hexapterygon, six-winged angel); it is uncertain whether they are <a href="/wiki/Seraph" title="Seraph">seraphim</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cherub" title="Cherub">cherubim</a>) located on the two east pendentives, covering their faces again before the end of the restoration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ho207_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-ho207-70">[70]</a></sup> The other two placed on the west pendentives are copies in paint created by the Fossatis, since they could find no surviving remains of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ho207_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-ho207-70">[70]</a></sup> As in this case, the architects reproduced in paint damaged decorative mosaic patterns, sometimes redesigning them in the process. The Fossati records are the primary sources about a number of mosaic images now believed to have been completely or partially destroyed in the <a href="/wiki/1894_Istanbul_earthquake" title="1894 Istanbul earthquake">1894 Istanbul earthquake</a>. These include a mosaic over a now-unidentified <i>Door of the Poor</i>, a large image of a jewel-encrusted <a href="/wiki/Cross" title="Cross">cross</a>, and a large number of images of <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">patriarchs</a>, and church fathers. Most of the missing images were located in the building's two tympana.</p>
<p>One mosaic they documented is <a href="/wiki/Christ_Pantocrator" title="Christ Pantocrator">Christ Pantocrator</a> in a circle, which would indicate it to be a ceiling mosaic, possibly even of the main dome which was later covered and painted over with Islamic calligraphy that expounds God as the light of the universe. The drawings of the Hagia Sophia mosaics are today kept in the Cantonal Archive of Ticino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="20th-century_restoration">20th-century restoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 20th-century restoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A large number of mosaics were uncovered in the 1930s by a team from the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Institute_of_America" title="Byzantine Institute of America">Byzantine Institute of America</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Whittemore" title="Thomas Whittemore">Thomas Whittemore</a>. The team chose to let a number of simple cross images remain covered by plaster, but uncovered all major mosaics found.</p>
<p>Because of its long history as both a church and a mosque, a particular challenge arises in the restoration process. Christian <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconographic</a> mosaics can be uncovered, but often at the expense of important and historic Islamic art. Restorers have attempted to maintain a balance between both Christian and Islamic cultures. In particular, much controversy rests upon whether the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic calligraphy</a> on the dome of the cathedral should be removed, in order to permit the underlying Pantocrator mosaic of Christ as Master of the World, to be exhibited (assuming the mosaic still exists).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_Gate_mosaic">Imperial Gate mosaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Imperial Gate mosaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Imperial_Gate_mosaic_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2208" data-file-width="3488" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Hagia_Sophia_Imperial_Gate_mosaic_2.jpg/220px-Hagia_Sophia_Imperial_Gate_mosaic_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Hagia_Sophia_Imperial_Gate_mosaic_2.jpg/330px-Hagia_Sophia_Imperial_Gate_mosaic_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Hagia_Sophia_Imperial_Gate_mosaic_2.jpg/440px-Hagia_Sophia_Imperial_Gate_mosaic_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Imperial_Gate_mosaic_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Imperial gate mosaic</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Imperial Gate mosaic is located in the <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a> above that gate, which was used only by the emperors when entering the church. Based on style analysis, it has been dated to the late 9th or early 10th century. The emperor with a <a href="/wiki/Halo_(religious_iconography)" title="Halo (religious iconography)">nimbus or halo</a> could possibly represent emperor <a href="/wiki/Leo_VI_the_Wise" title="Leo VI the Wise">Leo VI the Wise</a> or his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_VII_Porphyrogenitus" title="Constantine VII Porphyrogenitus">Constantine VII Porphyrogenitus</a> bowing down before Christ Pantocrator, seated on a jeweled throne, giving His blessing and holding in His left hand an open book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The text on the book reads as follows: "Peace be with you. I am the light of the world". (John 20:19; 20:26; 8:12) On each side of Christ's shoulders is a circular medallion: on His left the Archangel Gabriel, holding a <a href="/wiki/Staff_of_office" title="Staff of office">staff</a>, on His right His Mother Mary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southwestern_entrance_mosaic">Southwestern entrance mosaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Southwestern entrance mosaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2007" data-file-width="3132" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/220px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/330px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/440px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Southwestern entrance mosaic</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The southwestern entrance mosaic, situated in the tympanum of the southwestern entrance, dates from the reign of <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> It was rediscovered during the restorations of 1849 by the Fossatis. The Virgin sits on a throne without a back, her feet resting on a pedestal, embellished with precious stones. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_Jesus" title="Child Jesus">Child Christ</a> sits on her lap, giving His blessing and holding a scroll in His left hand. On her left side stands emperor Constantine in ceremonial attire, presenting a model of the city to Mary. The inscription next to him says: "Great emperor Constantine of the Saints". On her right side stands emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a>, offering a model of the Hagia Sophia. The medallions on both sides of the Virgin's head carry the <a href="/wiki/Monogram" title="Monogram">monograms</a> MP and ΘY, an abbreviation of "<i>Mētēr</i>" and "<i>Theou</i>", meaning "Mother of God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Apse_mosaics">Apse mosaics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Apse mosaics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apse_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia_Virgin_and_Child.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2948" data-file-width="2078" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Apse_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia_Virgin_and_Child.jpg/220px-Apse_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia_Virgin_and_Child.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Apse_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia_Virgin_and_Child.jpg/330px-Apse_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia_Virgin_and_Child.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Apse_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia_Virgin_and_Child.jpg/440px-Apse_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia_Virgin_and_Child.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apse_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia_Virgin_and_Child.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Apse mosaic of the Virgin and Child</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Virgin and Child mosaic was the first of the post-iconoclastic mosaics. It was inaugurated on 29 March 867 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Photios I of Constantinople">Patriarch Photius</a> and the emperors <a href="/wiki/Michael_III" title="Michael III">Michael III</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basil_I" title="Basil I">Basil I</a>. This mosaic is situated in a high location on the half dome of the apse. Mary is sitting on a throne without a back, holding the Child Jesus on her lap. Her feet rest on a pedestal. Both the pedestal and the throne are adorned with precious stones. The portraits of the archangels Gabriel and Michael (largely destroyed) in the <a href="/wiki/Bema" title="Bema">bema</a> of the arch also date from the 9th century. The mosaics are set against the original golden background of the 6th century. These mosaics were believed to be a reconstruction of the mosaics of the 6th century that were previously destroyed during the iconoclastic era by the Byzantines of that time, as represented in the inaugural sermon by the patriarch Photios. However, no record of figural decoration of Hagia Sophia exists before this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Emperor_Alexander_mosaic">Emperor Alexander mosaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Emperor Alexander mosaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Emperor Alexander mosaic is not easy to find for the first-time visitor, located in the second floor in a dark corner of the ceiling. It depicts <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_(emperor)" title="Alexander (emperor)">Emperor Alexander</a> in full regalia, holding a scroll in his right hand and a <a href="/wiki/Globus_cruciger" title="Globus cruciger">globus cruciger</a> in his left. A drawing by the Fossatis showed that the mosaic survived until 1849, and that <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Whittemore" title="Thomas Whittemore">Thomas Whittemore</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Institute_of_America" title="Byzantine Institute of America">Byzantine Institute of America</a> who was granted permission to preserve the mosaics, assumed that it had been destroyed in the earthquake of 1894. Eight years after his death, the mosaic was discovered in 1958 largely through the researches of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Van_Nice&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Robert Van Nice (page does not exist)">Robert Van Nice</a>. Unlike most of the other mosaics in Hagia Sophia, which had been covered over by ordinary plaster, the Alexander mosaic was simply painted over and reflected the surrounding mosaic patterns and thus was well hidden. It was duly cleaned by the Byzantine Institute's successor to Whittemore, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Paul_A._Underwood&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Paul A. Underwood (page does not exist)">Paul A. Underwood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Empress_Zoe_mosaic">Empress Zoe mosaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Empress Zoe mosaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empress_Zoe_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2060" data-file-width="2472" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Empress_Zoe_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/220px-Empress_Zoe_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Empress_Zoe_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/330px-Empress_Zoe_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Empress_Zoe_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/440px-Empress_Zoe_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empress_Zoe_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Empress Zoe mosaic</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Empress Zoe mosaic on the eastern wall of the southern gallery date from the 11th century. Christ Pantocrator, clad in the dark blue robe (as is the custom in Byzantine art), is seated in the middle against a golden background, giving His blessing with the right hand and holding the Bible in His left hand. On either side of His head are the monograms <i>IC</i> and <i>XC</i>, meaning <i>Iēsous Christos</i>. He is flanked by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_IX_Monomachus" title="Constantine IX Monomachus">Constantine IX Monomachus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoe_(empress)" title="Zoe (empress)">Empress Zoe</a>, both in ceremonial costumes. He is offering a purse, as symbol of the donation he made to the church, while she is holding a scroll, symbol of the donations she made. The inscription over the head of the emperor says: "Constantine, pious emperor in Christ the God, king of the Romans, Monomachus". The inscription over the head of the empress reads as follows: "Zoë, the very pious Augusta". The previous heads have been scraped off and replaced by the three present ones. Perhaps the earlier mosaic showed her first husband <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanos_III" title="Romanos III">Romanus III Argyrus</a> or her second husband <a href="/wiki/Michael_IV_the_Paphlagonian" title="Michael IV the Paphlagonian">Michael IV</a>. Another theory is that this mosaic was made for an earlier emperor and empress, with their heads changed into the present ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Comnenus_mosaic">Comnenus mosaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Comnenus mosaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comnenus_mosaics_Hagia_Sophia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1783" data-file-width="3386" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Comnenus_mosaics_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/220px-Comnenus_mosaics_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Comnenus_mosaics_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/330px-Comnenus_mosaics_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Comnenus_mosaics_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/440px-Comnenus_mosaics_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comnenus_mosaics_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Comnenus mosaic</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Comnenus mosaic, also located on the eastern wall of the southern gallery, dates from 1122. The Virgin Mary is standing in the middle, depicted, as usual in Byzantine art, in a dark blue gown. She holds the Child Christ on her lap. He gives His blessing with His right hand while holding a scroll in His left hand. On her right side stands emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_II_Comnenus" title="John II Comnenus">John II Comnenus</a>, represented in a garb embellished with precious stones. He holds a purse, symbol of an imperial donation to the church. Empress <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piroska_of_Hungary" title="Piroska of Hungary">Irene</a> stands on the left side of the Virgin, wearing ceremonial garments and offering a document. Their eldest son <a href="/wiki/Alexios_Komnenos_(co-emperor)" title="Alexios Komnenos (co-emperor)">Alexius Comnenus</a> is represented on an adjacent pilaster. He is shown as a beardless youth, probably representing his appearance at his coronation aged seventeen. In this panel one can already see a difference with the Empress Zoe mosaic that is one century older. There is a more realistic expression in the portraits instead of an idealized representation. The empress is shown with plaited blond hair, rosy cheeks and grey eyes, revealing her Hungarian descent. The emperor is depicted in a dignified manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="De.C3.ABsis_mosaic">Deësis mosaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Deësis mosaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2429" data-file-width="4176" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/220px-Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/330px-Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/440px-Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Deësis mosaic</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De%C3%ABsis" title="Deësis">Deësis</a> mosaic (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Δέησις</span>, "Entreaty") probably dates from 1261. It was commissioned to mark the end of 57 years of Roman Catholic use and the return to the Orthodox faith. It is the third panel situated in the imperial enclosure of the upper galleries. It is widely considered the finest in Hagia Sophia, because of the softness of the features, the humane expressions and the tones of the mosaic. The style is close to that of the Italian painters of the late 13th or early 14th century, such as <a href="/wiki/Duccio" title="Duccio">Duccio</a>. In this panel the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> (<i>Ioannes Prodromos</i>), both shown in three-quarters profile, are imploring the intercession of Christ Pantocrator for humanity on <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Judgment Day</a>. The bottom part of this mosaic is badly deteriorated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> This mosaic is considered as the beginning of the Renaissance in Byzantine pictorial art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_tympanum_mosaics">Northern tympanum mosaics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Northern tympanum mosaics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The northern <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a> mosaics feature various saints. They have been able to survive due to the very high and unreachable location. They depict Saints <a href="/wiki/John_Chrysostom" title="John Chrysostom">John Chrysostom</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Ignatius_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Ignatius of Constantinople">Ignatius the Younger</a> standing, clothed in white robes with crosses, and holding richly jeweled Holy Bibles. The names of each saint is given around the statues in Greek, in order to enable an identification for the visitor. The other mosaics in the other tympana have not survived probably due to the frequent earthquakes as opposed to any deliberate destruction by the Ottoman conquerors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_burials">Other burials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hagia_Sophia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Other burials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mustafa_I" title="Mustafa I">Mustafa I</a>, in the courtyard</li>
</ul>

<p><b>Related buildings:</b></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Apostles" title="Church of the Holy Apostles">Church of the Holy Apostles</a>—the second most important church of Constantinople</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hagia_Irene" title="Hagia Irene">Hagia Irene</a>—neighbouring church</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Little_Hagia_Sophia" title="Little Hagia Sophia">Little Hagia Sophia</a>—a 6th-century Byzantine church, now mosque, that might have been built by the same architects as Hagia Sophia</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chora_Church" title="Chora Church">Chora Church</a>—Byzantine church in Istanbul notable for its well preserved Paleologan mosaics, now a museum too</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pammakaristos_Church" title="Pammakaristos Church">Pammakaristos Church</a>: its parekklesion—also a museum—is decorated with beautiful mosaics</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Haseki_H%C3%BCrrem_Sultan_Hamam%C4%B1" title="Haseki Hürrem Sultan Hamamı">Haseki Hürrem Sultan Hamamı</a>—bath commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Roxelana" title="Roxelana">Roxelana</a> for the Hagia Sophia community</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cafera%C4%9Fa_Medresseh" title="Caferağa Medresseh">Caferağa Medresseh</a>—former Koranic school next to Hagia Sophia</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/So%C4%9Fuk%C3%A7e%C5%9Fme_Soka%C4%9F%C4%B1" title="Soğukçeşme Sokağı">Soğukçeşme Sokağı</a>—historical street between the Hagia Sophia and Topkapı Palace</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Sophia_Cathedral_in_Kiev" title="Saint Sophia Cathedral in Kiev">Saint Sophia Cathedral in Kiev</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Sophia_Cathedral_in_Novgorod" title="Saint Sophia Cathedral in Novgorod">Saint Sophia Cathedral in Novgorod</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saint_Sophia_Cathedral_in_Polotsk" title="Saint Sophia Cathedral in Polotsk">Saint Sophia Cathedral in Polotsk</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia_Church_(Sofia)" title="Hagia Sophia Church (Sofia)">Hagia Sophia Church (Sofia)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_the_National_Shrine_of_the_Immaculate_Conception" title="Basilica of the National Shrine of the Immaculate Conception">Basilica of the National Shrine of the Immaculate Conception</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franciscan_Monastery_(Washington,_DC)" title="Franciscan Monastery (Washington, DC)">Franciscan Monastery (Washington, DC)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saint_Clement_Catholic_Church,_Chicago" title="Saint Clement Catholic Church, Chicago">Saint Clement Catholic Church, Chicago</a>—the designer was said to have been influenced by the Hagia Sophia</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Catholic_Archeparchy_of_Philadelphia" title="Ukrainian Catholic Archeparchy of Philadelphia">Ukrainian Catholic Archeparchy of Philadelphia</a>—the article shows the cathedral, resembling the Hagia Sophia</li>
</ul>
